<span id="freeTextContainer1586576541489423999">THE STUNNING SEQUEL TO THE GOLDEN COMPASS<br />Having slipped through a newly formed astral portal, the intrepid Lyra finds herself in the beautiful, haunted world of Città gazze--a city where soul-eating Specters stalk the streets and the wingbeats of distant angels sound against the sky. But she is not without allies. For young Will Parry, in search of his father, has also stu</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1586576541489423999">THE STUNNING SEQUEL TO THE GOLDEN COMPASS<br />Having slipped through a newly formed astral portal, the intrepid Lyra finds herself in the beautiful, haunted world of Città gazze--a city where soul-eating Specters stalk the streets and the wingbeats of distant angels sound against the sky. But she is not without allies. For young Will Parry, in search of his father, has also stumbled into this strange new realm via a magic gateway.<br />Together the enlightened pair forge ahead on a perilous journey between worlds teeming with witches, angels, and sorcery--and uncover a deadly secret: an object of extraordinary and devastating power. But with every step, they move closer to an even greater threat--and the shattering truth of their own destiny . . .</span>

      newTip['wrapper'].addClassName('prototipAllowOverflow');

        $('edition_394364').observe('prototip:shown', function () {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function (e) {
            if ($('edition_394364').hasClassName('ignored')) {
              e.setStyle({'display': 'none'});
              return;
            }
            e.setStyle({'overflow': 'visible'});
          });
        });
      $('edition_394364').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

//]]&gt;
</script></div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown">
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_11614953">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/11614953" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/741421-dan" class="left imgcol" title="Dan"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1357230951p2/741421.jpg" alt="Dan" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/11614953?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 07, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/741421-dan" class="user" title="Dan">Dan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/119324.The_Subtle_Knife" title="Hardcover 9780679879251" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/741421-dan?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer11614953">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14984184220985963824">The second entry in a trilogy is often, in my opinion, the best. The author doesn't have to introduce the universe or the characters, as they did in the first installment, but they don't need to worry about wrapping up all the plot points either. Instead, the focus can be on 'the good stuff': elaborating on the story, teasing us more, giving action, chopping off Luke's hand and so on. Instead of the good stuff, in <em>The Subtle Knife</em> I feel as though we've had a bait and switch pulled on us.<br /><br />In <a title="The Golden Compass" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=The%20Golden%20Compass">The</a></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14984184220985963824">The second entry in a trilogy is often, in my opinion, the best. The author doesn't have to introduce the universe or the characters, as they did in the first installment, but they don't need to worry about wrapping up all the plot points either. Instead, the focus can be on 'the good stuff': elaborating on the story, teasing us more, giving action, chopping off Luke's hand and so on. Instead of the good stuff, in <em>The Subtle Knife</em> I feel as though we've had a bait and switch pulled on us.<br /><br />In <a title="The Golden Compass" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=The%20Golden%20Compass">The Golden Compass</a>, we were treated to a rich alternate universe that had elements that were similar to our own, like some of the geopolitical structure, and elements that were entirely fantastical, like armored polar bears and witches. <em>The Subtle Knife</em>, however decides that most of this is insignificant and takes place almost entirely in different universes. It seems like <a title="Philip Pullman" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=Philip%20Pullman">Philip Pullman</a> wanted to reel us in with fantasy before he could preach at us.<br /><br />Some of these elements are expanded upon in <a title="The Amber Spyglass" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=The%20Amber%20Spyglass">The Amber Spyglass</a>, which I'm currently reading, so forgive me if they don't all apply. I had heard before I started the series that they were 'about killing God.' This seemed highly unlikely, and was probably a knee-jerk reaction from people who heard it from other people who read a synopsis of the book, etc. But... no. Some of the main characters have decided to wage war on 'The Authority.'<br /><br />Herein lies my main concern with the series as a whole: it's not (excuse the pun) subtle. I'm an agnostic, so these complaints don't come from someone insulted by the material, they come from someone unhappy by their handling. I love plots that put a spin on traditional religion (<a title="Waiting for the Galactic Bus" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=Waiting%20for%20the%20Galactic%20Bus">Waiting for the Galactic Bus</a>, for example), but it seems like Pullman came up with a story involving a culture's religion and then decided to make it fit with the Judeo-Christian framework no matter how hard he had to push.<br /><br />The concept of Dust is interesting. Adapting it to fit with concepts of physics in our world works because it uses something we only know a little about. Once you try to toss in angels and consciousness and so on (which is insulting in a children's book, as he's claiming that children are entirely self involved until puberty), though, it seems contrived and silly.<br /><br />I may have been more willing to swallow his philosophy, such as it is, if there hadn't been a complete lack of the elements I liked in <em>The Golden Compass</em>: there were no Gyptians, there were no <em>panserbjörne</em>... they seem to make a reappearance in the final book of the series, but why spend so much time on their culture in the first book if you aren't going to include them in the second? (I know that the <em>panserbjörne</em>'s culture is basically that of any warrior society, but they're still armored polar bears and the ten year old in me think that's awesome)<br /><br />It's not so much that the book is bad, per se, though I do think it becomes too dark for the age group I initially thought it was written for. I just don't think Pullman is writing for the same reason I want to read: he wants to write religious commentary while I want to read fantasy.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14984184220985963824">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown">
    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_9948430">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/9948430" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/307208-lucy" class="left imgcol" title="Lucy"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1233177432p2/307208.jpg" alt="Lucy" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/9948430?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 02, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/307208-lucy" class="user" title="Lucy">Lucy</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/119324.The_Subtle_Knife" title="Hardcover 9780679879251" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer9948430">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14544660222576542290"><br />I am not a fan of forwarded emails. They frustrate me, because they usually come from the same group of people, people I like a great deal but who never send me a normal "hey, how's it going?" message. Just "Support our Troops" or "Tell every woman you know she's special" or "Microsoft is running a test and if you send this you could get a check for $1,000!" When I see the letters FWD in the subject line, I usually simply delete it.<br /><br />I lost track of the number of emails I received telling me about</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14544660222576542290"><br />I am not a fan of forwarded emails. They frustrate me, because they usually come from the same group of people, people I like a great deal but who never send me a normal "hey, how's it going?" message. Just "Support our Troops" or "Tell every woman you know she's special" or "Microsoft is running a test and if you send this you could get a check for $1,000!" When I see the letters FWD in the subject line, I usually simply delete it.<br /><br />I lost track of the number of emails I received telling me about the Anti-God movie The Golden Compass and the need to boycott the movies and the books. It was well over ten. Ten people wanted me to send that email on to everyone I know, telling them the same thing. Don't see this movie! Don't read these books! Alert! Alert! Alert!<br /><br />Nothing like calling attention to something like a planned boycott. I haven't seen the movie, because I'm cheap and it's not something I'd take my kids to or something I'd be prone to see anyway, but as I had already read the first book in the series, enjoyed it and hadn't found it to be Anti-God, I was curious to read the next to see what the fuss was all about.<br /><br />I don't know if the emails worked and I read with a bias, but I did not enjoy this second book. Not because it is Anti-God...which it really isn't, but more anti organized religion and organized religion's version of god, but because the writing is bad.<br /><br />Dialogue - choppy.<br /><br />Descriptions - cliched. (how do I get that little accent marking over the e?)<br /><br />Storyline - totally falters.<br /><br />I felt enormously disappointed in the direction of this book. Lyra, the young female protagonist in search of dust and her father, all but disappears in this second story. She still plays a part, but now as the sidekick to Will, a new character who is a giant "young adult fiction" stereotype (in search of the father he never knew while protecting his mother from bad guys and seems to be gifted in the combat department). I don't remember the writing being bad in the first book. I thought it descriptive and unique and thoughtful. Not so, in The Subtle Knife. Pullman changes gears and loses focus. There is a lot more going on and none of it is developed well. I stopped caring about the characters and their goals.<br /><br />I think these books had great potential. There could be a lot to discuss with adolescents (not young children...at all). The nature of the soul, the natural man, the costs and benefits of religions. All appropriate things to discuss with youth ready to question and discover on their own.<br /><br />Pullman takes that conversation away with his lack of metaphor. It becomes impossible to argue, "I think the dust means this." or "what do you think The Authority is for Pullman?" when he throws his opinion at you with real life Christian beliefs. It's inappropriate and unfair. Write a fantasy or a satire or a parable if you want to. Other authors have done it...and done it well.<br /><br />Pullman didn't.<br /><br />I won't be reading the third book.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14544660222576542290">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_23247922">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/23247922" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1196959-candace-wynell-mchann" class="left imgcol" title="Candace Wynell McHann"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1426349160p2/1196959.jpg" alt="Candace Wynell McHann" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/23247922?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 29, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1196959-candace-wynell-mchann" class="user" title="Candace Wynell McHann">Candace Wynell McHann</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/119324.The_Subtle_Knife" title="Hardcover 9780679879251" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a onclick="Effect.toggle('reviewTextContainer23247922'); return false;" href="#">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span style="display:none" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer23247922">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16634263373970473727">Way back at the end of November/beginning of December of good ol' 2007, I read Phillip Pullman's The Golden Compass. Why? Well, because there was all that talk about how it got made into a movie. I read the book, and found it very thought provoking. As for the movie it's a watered down version of Pullman's work, but not bad for the most part. I mean, I don't think it would go over too well with audiences who haven't read to book to know that Lyra's friend Roger is killed by Lord Asreil and that</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16634263373970473727">Way back at the end of November/beginning of December of good ol' 2007, I read Phillip Pullman's The Golden Compass. Why? Well, because there was all that talk about how it got made into a movie. I read the book, and found it very thought provoking. As for the movie it's a watered down version of Pullman's work, but not bad for the most part. I mean, I don't think it would go over too well with audiences who haven't read to book to know that Lyra's friend Roger is killed by Lord Asreil and that Mrs. Coulter actually wants to control Lyra and in The Subtle Knife decide that yeah, she has to die. <br /><br />Just for a quick overview of Phillip Pullman's contraversal work and why it's a contraversy. There are three books in the series called, His Dark Material: The Golden Compass, The Subtle Knife, and The Amber Spyglass. The title of the series comes from a phrase Milton uses in Paradise Lost, which for those who don't know is a 16th century epic poem about Satan's great Fall from Heaven and the mischief he does in getting Adam and Eve to commit Original Sin. The books are about a rebellious girl named Lyra Belacqua, who comes from a world like the one we know, but is also very different. I guess you could say it's a warped version of the Victorian period. In Lyra's world, people have "deamons," which are external forms of people's soul in the form of animals(Lyra's daemon is named Pantalaimon). She gets a hold of a golden compass, a device called an alethiometer, which at first she doesn't know how to use, but soon finds that it allows her to see into the past, present, and future. There is also the issue of a substance called Dust, which surronds all human life. The Church, also called the Magistrium, a very tyrannical and oppressive hand that acts first and questions later, wants to destroy Dust and Lord Asriel (who we think is Lyra's uncle but, da da da!! it's actually her father). Mrs. Coulter, being a very powerful figure in the Church, tries to seduce Lyra in a world of privilge but soon realizes that Mrs. Coulter is trying to control her. Lyra, being a free spirit, doesn't take kindly to this. So she runs away, meeting up with gyptians, where she learns to read the althieometer; gets badass texan named Lee Scoresby (and his daemon Hester)talking armored bear named Iorek Byrnison to work with her; she gets kidnapped by Tarters and sent to Bolvanger discovers the sinister secret of the Church: they cut daemons and children apart in a process known as intercision and finds that Mrs. Coulter (who is da da da!! her mother!) is in charge of the whole operation; plans an escape for all the childern where she mets the witch queen, Serafina Pekkela, and the queen helps Lyra on her quest to find her father; and her friend Roger is killed by her own father Lord Asriel which results in the creatation of a bridge from his world into another. The story ends with a very pissed off Lyra following Lord Asriel into the new world.<br /><br />So yeah, a lot to take in. I just gave an overview! There are so many carefully thought out details that I have left out. For example: as a child, their daemon's have the ability to change from animal to animal. When the child reaches to adulthood, their daemons settle into one animal. Very cool right? <br /><br />So, what's the contraversy all about?<br /><br />One: Phillip Pullman is an out spoken atheist.<br />Two: His Dark Materials are catagorized as childrens books. <br />Three: You find out in The Subtle Knife that the big plan is to kill God.<br /><br />Yeah. <br /><br />The Subtle Knife introduces Will, who stummbles across a window to city called Cittàgazze on another world. There he meets Lyra. I would explain more, but you've already put up with me talking about the first book, so let me sum up for you by what the back of the book says:<br />"In this stunning sequel to The Golden Compass, the intrepid Lyra find herself in the simmering, hauinted otherworld-cittagazze, where soul-eating Specters stalk the streets and wingsbeats of angels sound against the sky. But she in not without allies: twelve-year-old Will Parry, feeling for his life after taking another's, has also stumbled into this strange new realm. On a perilous journey from world to world, Lyra and Will uncover a deadly secret: an object of extraordinary and devasting power. And with every step,they move closer to an even greater threat-and the shattering truth of their own destiny."<br />I know you want to read it now just see what happens. Anyway, it's very amazing and disturbing and fantasic and all those things. <br /><br />I just finished reading The Subtle Knife today so I have yet to read The Amber Spyglass. When I do, I'll let you know.<br /><br />But why are these books good and why is Pullman on my Influential Writers list along with Donne, Plath, and Hemingway? Because he wrote a children's book version of Milton's Paradise Lost! Have you ever read it? Holy Crap! I had to read it twice while I was in college. Don't get me wrong. It's awesome. It is awe inspiring. Everyone should read it. But damnit, it's long and can be a little hard to read if you don't have someone there explaining it or Cliff Notes. In Milton's poem, Satan leads an army of rebellious angels in an attempt to overthrow God. The attempt fails, and Satan and his followers are cast out of heaven. Satan, seeking revenge, convinces God's creations, Adam and Eve, to eat the fruit of the tree of knowledge, thus causing their fall from grace. In Pullman's take on Paradise Lost, God is an oppressive, senile old man, and Satan is a dashing heroic figure. But the real hero and centerpiece of Pullman's story is the Eve figure, Lyra Belacqua, on whom the salvation of the universe depends.<br /><br />Really? A children's book? Wow! That's what I said. So I raise my glass of Cranberry-Pomagrante Crystal Lite and vodka to Phillip Pullman for being the writer I want to be: original, brave, and honest. <br /><br />And to go off an a rant real quick:<br />How can people protest and get all up in arms when they haven't even read/watched what they are getting upset about? Understandably, if you read Pullman's His Dark Materials, and you are against it, awesome. You know what are you talking about. I got really upset when people started attacking the movie without having read the book or even seen the movie. How do I know? Well, because I asked. Are people so afraid to have their ideology put into question? Do they not want to think critically, analyze what they believe in? I love being challenged. Good lord, what else do I have to do intellectualy until I light a fire under my ass and get my teaching certificate? It's okay to question what your faith and your beliefs. It allows you to see how strong you are in the decisions you have made.<br /><br />Sorry about that last rant there...but that had been building up for a while. Not just from Pullman's work, but also from a few other things that I would talk about but this blog is already long enough.<br /><br />Well, I hope you enjoyed me getting my nerd on. It was a blast!<br /><br />Ciao, darlings!!<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16634263373970473727">...more</a>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1335003744?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 09, 2016</a>

      <a href="/user/show/25821784-xime-garc-a" class="user" title="Xime García">Xime García</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/2225234.La_Daga" title="Hardcover 9788466636247" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/25821784-xime-garc-a?shelf=love-of-my-life" class="actionLinkLite">love-of-my-life</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/25821784-xime-garc-a?shelf=rese%C3%B1ados" class="actionLinkLite">reseñados</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1335003744">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6361881338822980844"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1455505589">Reseña de "Luces del Norte" (La Materia Oscura 1)</a><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1499827524">Reseña de "El Catalejo Lacado" (La Materia Oscura 3)</a><br /><br /><blockquote> <em>¿Habéis intervenido en la evolución humana?<br /><pre>SÍ.</pre> <br />¿Por qué?<br /><pre>VENGANZA.</pre></em> </blockquote><br /><br /><strong>Este segundo libro</strong> me gustó muchísimo más que el primero. Se dieron muchos giros, conocimos nuevos personajes y arribamos a nuevos mundos. Todo lo planteado en <em>Luces del Norte</em> gana mayor terreno (diría que <em>colosal</em>) y todo lo que pensábamos que podía ser de una forma, termina siendo de otra.<br /><br />Sin embargo, puedo ver por qué hay alguno</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6361881338822980844"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1455505589">Reseña de "Luces del Norte" (La Materia Oscura 1)</a><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1499827524">Reseña de "El Catalejo Lacado" (La Materia Oscura 3)</a><br /><br /><blockquote>
  <em>¿Habéis intervenido en la evolución humana?<br /><pre>SÍ.</pre>
<br />¿Por qué?<br /><pre>VENGANZA.</pre></em>
</blockquote><br /><br /><strong>Este segundo libro</strong> me gustó muchísimo más que el primero. Se dieron muchos giros, conocimos nuevos personajes y arribamos a nuevos mundos. Todo lo planteado en <em>Luces del Norte</em> gana mayor terreno (diría que <em>colosal</em>) y todo lo que pensábamos que podía ser de una forma, termina siendo de otra.<br /><br />Sin embargo, puedo ver por qué hay algunos que no gustaron de esta saga, porque es con este libro con el que la historia despega hacia una dirección totalmente opuesta a la que creíamos (o no quisimos ver) que iba a tener. En mi opinión personal, <strong>se pone mejor</strong> y lo que nos espera en la última parte de la trilogía tiene que ser <strong>magnánimo</strong>.<br /><br />En resumen:<br /><br /><strong>
  <pre>    <blockquote>LO MEJOR:</blockquote>
  </pre>
</strong><br /><strong>
  <u>El argumento.</u>
</strong> Sin duda, este libro fue lo que los yanquis llaman un <em>roller coaster of emotions</em>, y no fue solamente el final, o los capítulos anteriores, sino que es todo el libro un continuo torrente de sucesos, información, descubrimientos y giros que me dejaron sin aire. En más de una ocasión me quedaba mirando la hoja con una tremenda cara de feliz cumpleaños y suspirando un "¡Aaaaaaaaahhhh!" de sorpresa. <br /><br /><strong>
  <pre>    <blockquote>LO BUENO:</blockquote>
  </pre>
</strong><br /><strong>
  <u>Los nuevos personajes.</u>
</strong> Amé a Will, y no puedo concebir mejor compañero y complemento a Lyra que él. Con tan solo sus acciones nos damos cuenta de cómo es: un guerrero, como bien dicen, independiente, maduro para su edad y, sobre todas las cosas, valiente. Me gusta cómo en ningún momento se lo describe (a diferencia de en el libro anterior en el que muchos personajes se decantaban diciendo lo inteligente o diferente que era Lyra) pero lo bien que el pequeño nuevo protagonista demuestra cómo es. En este caso, lo de que una imagen dice más que mil palabras se aplica perfectamente. <br /><br /><strong>
  <pre>    <blockquote>LO MALO:</blockquote>
  </pre>
</strong><br /><strong>
  <u>El cliffhanger, y algunas inverosimilitudes.</u>
</strong> Me sorprende la confianza con la que algunos personajes reciben a desconocidos y cuentan todo lo que saben (me refiero a más de una ocasión), y también me asombra la rapidez con la que los protagonistas consiguen respuestas. Sin embargo, fue todo tan agobiante y tan abrumador, que lo dejo pasar, porque la narración de Pullman se disfruta mucho, y hasta me parece que se vuelve mejor conforme avanzan los libros. <br /><br /><strong>
  <pre>    <blockquote>LO PEOR:</blockquote>
  </pre>
</strong><br /><strong>
  <u>Los cambios,</u>
</strong> que en mi parecer no fueron malos (FUERON GLORIOSOS), pero pueden levantar controversia en los lectores. Están advertidos todos aquellos que comenzaron con <em>Luces del Norte</em> y pretenden seguir la saga, que esto <strong>ya no es fantasía, sino que pasó a un plano totalmente religioso o metafísico.</strong> Insisto en que esta no puede ser una saga para niños, tocando temas tan delicados (que trajeron sus problemas al autor según pude leer un poco en Wikipedia sin spoilearme) y explorándolos a sus anchas sin ninguna regla ni restricción. Porque eso mismo es lo que hace Pullman: agarra el cristianismo y te lo retuerce delante de tu cara. Fuerte. <br />Para aquellos que buscaban una simple saga de fantasía, tal vez estos libros no sean lo que esperaban. Más de una persona me dijo que la historia derivaba en algo totalmente distinto a lo que se esperaba desde un principio, y una amiga (ya lejana, por desgracia) se los había comprado hace unos años y luego los revendió porque "no eran lo que había pensado". Ahora comprendo todo. <br /><br /><img width="400" height="560" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1452204087i/17661311._SY540_.jpg" /><br /><br />Para aquellos interesados en continuar, creo que es una saga que vale la pena. Yo sinceramente no esperaba nada, ni fantasía ni metafísica, y por eso el rumbo que está tomando no me disgusta en absoluto. De hecho me encanta, pues me siento bastante "letrada", si pudiera decirse, en el tema, y disfruto sin prejuicios de historias de este estilo, cuando están bien hechas. <br /><br /><blockquote>
  <em>—Existen dos grandes poderes —declaró— que se enfrentan desde el comienzo de los tiempos. Todo avance en la vida del hombre, todo jirón de conocimiento, sabiduría y decencia que poseemos se lo ha arrancado de los dientes un bando al otro. <strong>Cada pequeño incremento en la libertad humana se ha conseguido a costa de una lucha feroz entre quienes desean que sepamos más y seamos más sabios y fuertes y quienes pretenden que obedezcamos y seamos humildes y sumisos</strong>.</em>
</blockquote></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6361881338822980844">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1330548713?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 04, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/21081005-antonio" class="user" title="Antonio">Antonio</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1330548713">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2199040353514038400"><strong>¡MALDITOS <u>CLIFFHANGERS!!!!!</u></strong> <br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1423517527ra/13637307.gif" /><br /><br />Repetiré lo que dije en la reseña de La brújula dorada la cual pueden ver <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/786385286?book_show_action=false"> aquí</a> estos no me parecen libros infantiles, no se que se le metió al editor o a quien se lo ocurrió la brillante idea de clasificarlos de esa forma, pero a mi parecer que tengamos por protagonista a niños no hace que el libro ipso facto sea infantil. Acá se siguen tratando temas complicados, aunque esta vez el centro de la historia parezca <strong>la búsqueda del padre de Will</strong>, una vez mas Pullman nos</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2199040353514038400"><strong>¡MALDITOS <u>CLIFFHANGERS!!!!!</u></strong> <br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1423517527ra/13637307.gif" /><br /><br />Repetiré lo que dije en la reseña de La brújula dorada la cual pueden ver <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/786385286?book_show_action=false"> aquí</a> estos no me parecen libros infantiles, no se que se le metió al editor o a quien se lo ocurrió la brillante idea de clasificarlos de esa forma, pero a mi parecer que tengamos por protagonista a niños no hace que el libro ipso facto sea infantil. Acá se siguen tratando temas complicados, aunque esta vez el centro de la historia parezca <strong>la búsqueda del padre de Will</strong>, una vez mas Pullman nos muestra que su trama es mucho mas complicada de lo que aparenta ser. <br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1436942714i/15520637._SX540_.jpg" /><br /><br />Los puentes entre los mundos se han abierto, el caos ha comenzado y por si fuera poco se esta preparando la mas terrible de todas la guerras del universo, decir que es una guerra entre el bien y el mal estaría totalmente errado, pues las cosas no son tan simples...<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <em>—Existen dos grandes poderes —declaró— que se enfrentan desde el comienzo de los tiempos. Todo avance en la vida del hombre, todo jirón de conocimiento, sabiduría y decencia que poseemos se lo ha arrancado de los dientes un bando al otro. Cada pequeño incremento en la libertad humana se ha conseguido a costa de una lucha feroz entre quienes desean que sepamos más y seamos más sabios y fuertes y quienes pretenden que obedezcamos y seamos humildes y sumisos.</em>
</blockquote><br /><br />...aunque si puedo decir que <strong>Lord Asriel</strong> esta formando y comandando un ejercito como nunca antes se ha visto, con seres y guerreros de todos los mundos, pero ¿A que se enfrenta? ¿Quién lidera el otro bando? nada menos que <strong>Dios</strong><br /><br />¿todavía siguen pensando que es un libro infantil? <br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1436942714ra/15520638.gif" /><br /><br />Mientras todo esto sucede <strong>Lyra</strong> perdida en un nuevo mundo conoce a <strong>Will</strong> (un chico de nuestro mundo) juntos tendrán que atravesar una serie de dificultades entablando amistad en el proceso. <br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1436942714i/15520639._SY540_.jpg" /><br /><br />Quizás existan varias razones por las que este libro no deba tener las cinco estrellas, pero la expansión del <strong>worldbuilding ahora siendo un multiverso, mas las nuevas incorporaciones como los espantos, los ángeles, los chamanes y la daga sutil, y la antesala de la guerra neo-apocalíptica me hace amar este libro</strong>, eso sin mencionar los conflictos religiosos y científicos, la violencia realista (tanto en adultos como en niños), las insinuaciones sexuales, y el drama filosófico y claro mas trama sobre las brujas <br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1381320270ra/4701140.gif" /><br /><br />Lo que no puedo perdonar es el maldito cliffhanger, osea puedo pasar el cambio en la narración, la nueva personalidad sumisa de Lyra, la falta de osos polares, las "casualidades" de la trama, pero el cliffhanger es maligno... esperen... yo ya tengo el tercer libro... puedo comenzarlo inmediatamente... <strong>
  <em>¡ja! ¡jodete cliffhanger!</em>
</strong> <br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1436942714ra/15520640.gif" /></span>

  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/179317938" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5686305-shayantani-das" class="left imgcol" title="Shayantani Das"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1436246159p2/5686305.jpg" alt="Shayantani Das" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/179317938?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 18, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5686305-shayantani-das" class="user" title="Shayantani Das">Shayantani Das</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/70948.The_Subtle_Knife" title="Paperback 9780590112895" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5686305-shayantani-das?shelf=favorite-protagonist" class="actionLinkLite">favorite-protagonist</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5686305-shayantani-das?shelf=social-issues" class="actionLinkLite">social-issues</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5686305-shayantani-das?shelf=supernatural-fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">supernatural-fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5686305-shayantani-das?shelf=coming-off-age" class="actionLinkLite">coming-off-age</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer179317938">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5307097962730858340">Two very strange things happened last week. I gave <a title="I Am Half Sick Of Shadows (Flavia De Luce, #4) by Alan Bradley" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11277218.I_Am_Half_Sick_Of_Shadows__Flavia_De_Luce___4_">I Am Half Sick Of Shadows: A Flavia De Luce Novel</a> two stars and am now giving this book five star. It is strange because the former book’s protagonist, my dear Flavia De Luce is my favorite obstinate pre teen. On the contrary, Lyra, another stubborn, precocious, pre-teen absolutely annoyed me in the previous book. Right now though, I can not for the life of me imagine why I did not like the first novel and Lyra. Well, at least I adore her right</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5307097962730858340">Two very strange things happened last week. I gave <a title="I Am Half Sick Of Shadows (Flavia De Luce, #4) by Alan Bradley" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11277218.I_Am_Half_Sick_Of_Shadows__Flavia_De_Luce___4_">I Am Half Sick Of Shadows: A Flavia De Luce Novel</a> two stars and am now giving this book five star. It is strange because the former book’s protagonist, my dear Flavia De Luce is my favorite obstinate pre teen. On the contrary, Lyra, another stubborn, precocious, pre-teen absolutely annoyed me in the previous book. Right now though, I can not for the life of me imagine why I did not like the first novel and Lyra. Well, at least I adore her right now.<br /><br />Philip Pullman is a genius. I loved every aspect of this book, the concept, the characterization, the plot, the pacing, everything. I remember thinking that the last book was rather boring, but this part had me catching my breath. Specters, rebel angels, parallel universe, golden compasses and subtle knives, daemons and witches- each chapter keeps you hooked and also provide fodder for the brain to muse about. I love the anti CS Lewis atmosphere of the book. <br /><br />The characters were another high point in this novel. Lyra Belacqua reappears with her daemon Pantalaimon and although she doesn’t play as central a role as the previous novel, she is absolutely charming. I can not wait to find out the form Pantalaimon will finally take. Marisa Coulter and Serafina Pekkala are back and as interesting as ever. Lee Scoresby and his daemon are absolutely heart breaking. A bandwagon of new and amazing characters like Stanislaus Grumman, Will Parry, Mary Malone is introduced. The friendship between Will and Lyra and Will and Pantalaimon were some of the best parts of the story. I would read the next book just to meet all these characters again. <br /><br />I am so glad I already have a copy of the next part. After that abrupt ending and the revelation about Lyra towards the end, having to wait for the next part would have been sheer torture. I never thought I would say this, but Flavia really is in grave danger of being dethroned as my favorite. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5307097962730858340">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_366621922">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/366621922" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4014494-lina" class="left imgcol" title="Lina"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1313620406p2/4014494.jpg" alt="Lina" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/366621922?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 17, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4014494-lina" class="user" title="Lina">Lina</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/18119.The_Subtle_Knife" title="Paperback 9780440238140" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/4014494-lina?shelf=children-fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">children-fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4014494-lina?shelf=classic-lit" class="actionLinkLite">classic-lit</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4014494-lina?shelf=children-books" class="actionLinkLite">children-books</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4014494-lina?shelf=wanted-to-love-but-didn-t" class="actionLinkLite">wanted-to-love-but-didn-t</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4014494-lina?shelf=sequel-bait-ending" class="actionLinkLite">sequel-bait-ending</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4014494-lina?shelf=alternate-reality" class="actionLinkLite">alternate-reality</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer366621922">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15765462985976864114">For a moment, just imagine that after reading <em>Harry Potter and the Sorcerer's Stone</em> you were so enthralled by the protagonist (even if you weren't bare with me), his friends and the entire world that has been established. It has moral undertones, but at it's heart it is a fun fantasy novel. Then you pick up <em>Harry Potter and the Chamber of Secrets</em> and suddenly Harry has been downgraded as a protagonist in favor of Dan, our brand new second main character. He's super awesome and whatnot and sort o</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15765462985976864114">For a moment, just imagine that after reading <em>Harry Potter and the Sorcerer's Stone</em> you were so enthralled by the protagonist (even if you weren't bare with me), his friends and the entire world that has been established. It has moral undertones, but at it's heart it is a fun fantasy novel. Then you pick up <em>Harry Potter and the Chamber of Secrets</em> and suddenly Harry has been downgraded as a protagonist in favor of Dan, our brand new second main character. He's super awesome and whatnot and sort of leeches attention away from Harry. Also the relationship between Harry, Ron and Hermione has been almost removed to the point that when see Ron or Hermione appear you suddenly remember "oh yeah" that person. On top of that, the moral undertones are now hammers that are pounded at you. It's not horrible, but after reading the first book, this is not what you imagined it would become. That is how I feel about The Subtle Knife. I did not hate this book, but after the awesomeness that was The Golden Compass, I'm really disappointed in this new world that we were brought into and the changes made to how the story was constructed.<br /><br />I thought that this was going to be Lyra's story, but it was taken over by Will and this really clumsy world building that I just could not enjoy as much as the previous book. I also was not a fan of all the different perspectives that were going on. In the previous novel they did a great job of putting Lyra is situations that advanced the plot and allowed her to grow as a character and we don't get that here. It makes everything come off as sloppy.<br /><br />That being said, this was not horrible, it was just a lackluster sequel. I am legitimately surprised that this is the highest ranked book in the series. Onward to Book 3.<br /><br />Oh and we get it...down with the establishment enough with the sermon.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15765462985976864114">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <a href="/book/show/119324.The_Subtle_Knife" title="Hardcover 9780679879251" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/27788046-bookworm-sean?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/27788046-bookworm-sean?shelf=sci-fi" class="actionLinkLite">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/27788046-bookworm-sean?shelf=children-of-all-ages" class="actionLinkLite">children-of-all-ages</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1110169977">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14996371216051548906"><strong>"All through that day the witches came, like flakes of black snow on the wings of a storm, filling the skies with the darting flutter of their silk and the swish of air through the needles of their cloud-pine branches. Men who hunted in the dripping forests or fished among melting ice-floes heard the sky-wide whisper through the fog, and if the sky was clear they would look up to see the witches flying, like scraps of darkness drifting on a secret tide."</strong><br /><br /><img width="400" height="250" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/photos/1416432171p8/1092593.jpg" /><br /><br />The first book was told, almost, exclusive</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14996371216051548906"><strong>"All through that day the witches came, like flakes of black snow on the wings of a storm, filling the skies with the darting flutter of their silk and the swish of air through the needles of their cloud-pine branches. Men who hunted in the dripping forests or fished among melting ice-floes heard the sky-wide whisper through the fog, and if the sky was clear they would look up to see the witches flying, like scraps of darkness drifting on a secret tide."</strong><br /><br /><img width="400" height="250" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/photos/1416432171p8/1092593.jpg" /><br /><br />The first book was told, almost, exclusively from the view point of one character: Lyra. This book, however, is from multiple points of views. I don’t think this works too well, after having the original book centre on one character; it made the novel feel as if it was in a different series. Lyra has three chapters told from her person. The same amount is told from the perspective of Will as he encountered Lyra. The rest of the chapters are from side characters of the previous book. It is a weird authorial decision to make, considering that one character was the focal point of the first book.<br /><br />The second protagonist of the series, the Adam to Pullaman’s Eve, takes the lead of this book. I have mixed feeling about this. As a character he is well rounded and relatable enough, it’s just….. why have him be the lead for the story. Why not simply have Lyra narrate the story as before? From the events of the Golden Compass, the author has established a strong heroine; a character that the reader grows to adore. So why add a new one?<br /><br />The plot of this book was very poor and nowhere near as accomplished as the one in The Golden Compass. No real closure was reached as this book seemed to be a mere set-up for the next instalment. This did not read like a second book in a series; it was lacking so many aspects; it was almost as if this book just had a middle with no end or beginning to accompany it. <br /><br />Paradise Lost has clearly inspired the author in the writing of this book, though it’s like this idea has only been considered in the second book. There is very little in regard to Adam and Eve’s downfall or that of Satan in the Golden Compass. <br /><br />Had I not such high regard for the first book, I would abandon this series, much as it pains me to say so. I loved the first book; it was one of the best books I’ve read. I sincerely hope that the next book in the series is more akin to the first than the second; otherwise, I might just try to remove it from my memory. My rating of this book may change; it depends on what I think of the last book in the series.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14996371216051548906">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span id="like_container_review_326123837" class="likeItContainer"><a href="/rating/voters/326123837?resource_type=Review" rel="nofollow" id="like_count_review_326123837"><span class="likesCount">16 likes</span></a> · <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F77473.The_Subtle_Knife" class="jsLike like_it gr-button gr-button--small" rel="nofollow">Like</a></span> · <a href="/review/show/326123837?book_show_action=true&amp;from_review_page=1">see review</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_32228274">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/32228274" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1425694-c" class="left imgcol" title="C."><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1245484320p2/1425694.jpg" alt="C." /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/32228274?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 10, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1425694-c" class="user" title="C.">C.</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/119324.The_Subtle_Knife" title="Hardcover 9780679879251" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1425694-c?shelf=to-be-re-read" class="actionLinkLite">to-be-re-read</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1425694-c?shelf=i-wish-i-own" class="actionLinkLite">i-wish-i-own</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1425694-c?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer32228274">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14765988749738480472">When I first read this book I was young enough to still pronounce the 'b' in 'subtle', and now I can't look at this book without doing it again. I still think it sounds better that way - it gives the word a sort of dull power that I think depicts the mysterious magic of the knife much better than the silly, flippant 'suttle'. Saying 'sub-tle' opens up previously-unimagined worlds which extend indefinitely into the distance. <br /><br />And this is what is good about this series. I've come up with a list of</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14765988749738480472">When I first read this book I was young enough to still pronounce the 'b' in 'subtle', and now I can't look at this book without doing it again. I still think it sounds better that way - it gives the word a sort of dull power that I think depicts the mysterious magic of the knife much better than the silly, flippant 'suttle'. Saying 'sub-tle' opens up previously-unimagined worlds which extend indefinitely into the distance. <br /><br />And this is what is good about this series. I've come up with a list of the main qualities that stand out in the fantasy books I enjoy, which is as follows:<br /><br />1. Engaging, complex characters combined with a realistic, convincing plot.<br />2. A tragedy so heart-rending that it is somehow beautiful.<br />3. An intriguing world.<br /><br />The fantasy books I have enjoyed most over the years all seem to have at least one of these characteristics. The first point, obviously, is not unique to fantasy, but I had to include it because there are a few fantasy books that I have read where magic and so on are fairly irrelevant. They are not the centre of the plot; you get the impression they were included simply because the author wanted to give themselves a different set of specifications, as a challenge perhaps, or maybe they felt bored or limited by the confines of reality. Example of this kind of book include anything by Diana Wynne Jones, as well as Robin Hobb's <em>Assassins</em> trilogy, which doesn't have either of the other two qualities, though the <em>Liveship Traders</em> trilogy that follows it has plenty of the third point.<br /><br />The second point relates to books whose denouement hinges on an event so sad that it is unutterably perfect, and you knew it could never be any other way. It was so sad that it was beautiful and the reason you cried was as much for that beauty as for the tragedy. The main books I can think of that have this quality are Cecilia Dart-Thornton's <em>Bitterbynde</em> trilogy and Sheri S. Tepper's <em>Beauty</em>, and there was another one by Juliet Marillier (I think). A disproportionate amount of Celtic mythology in there, anyway. But: I haven't read any of these books for years, so it's quite possible that my obsession with their tragedy was just a product of adolescent romanticism. But anyway.<br /><br />The third point is, I believe, the most important for fantasy. Books that are set in the equivalent of a medieval England with magic would be boring if that was all there was to it, so they have to have at least one of the other two points to be a good book. The truly great fantasy books are the ones that make you want to know more about the world in which they are set. A single novel is really quite a small number of words to depict an entire world, and I hope no author would be stupid enough to try it. The ones who really have talent can suggest, though, in just a few chapters, enough to inspire a fascination in you that will never leave. The word 'intriguing' is nowhere near strong enough to describe it. Garth Nix's <em>Abhorsen</em> trilogy is one of these, and Susannah Clarke's <em>Jonathan Strange and Mr Norrell</em> is the queen of them all, and probably my favourite fantasy book.<br /><br /><em>His Dark Materials</em> is a book of the third point, though it has elements of the first and second points too. Pullman's genius is to create a world that is very similar to ours, and then say that in fact there are infinite numbers of other worlds that are also very similar to ours, and that they all overlay each other, and that it is possible to travel between them. The contrasts between the different worlds allow him to make much of all sorts of different ways of looking at something. It's important to note that no one in these books ever thinks of what they are doing as magic - it is science, or it is religion. It is theoretical physics and experimental theology. The lines between science and religion and magic, three things that are not supposed to intersect, are blurred beyond all recognition.<br /><br />It's not badly written, but the best thing you can say of the prose is that it never distracts, which in this case is perfect. And of course, the characters are all right. Will and Lyra are cool, but despite their so-called 'flaws' they're also brave and good and perfect in a rather boring way. The villains aren't bad, and I like the way there are groups of characters, such as the bears and the witches, whose societies work differently to ours and who think differently to us and who do things differently as a result. The clashes between these different ways of thought (such as the one that leads to the death of Jopari (attempting to avoid spoilers)) are very well done. But nothing is really out of the ordinary.<br /><br />Most important, though, are the daemons. I don't think it does anything special character-wise - it's not a radically new way of creating personality or anything - but it does have a lot of tragic potential (think Lee Scoresby and Hester in the ravine, trying to call Serafina Pekkala to save them), and it is a crucial part of what makes the world so intriguing. That one's soul can live outside of oneself is something, and that this soul can then be separated from oneself is something else. That this soul can be eaten by strange spectral beings, whether within the body or without, is another thing again. That there is a mysterious substance that is attracted to this soul, and that this strange attraction varies according to the age of a person, that is brilliant.<br /><br />I think what I like about Pullman is that he takes his ideas much further than would occur to most other people. And somehow you can sense, like the alethiometers' invisible layers of meaning extending down to negative infinity, that the ideas ramify back endlessly too, and all you have to do is think about it and you can get there. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14765988749738480472">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_103744127">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/103744127" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2658311-notgettingenough" class="left imgcol" title="notgettingenough "><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1258115161p2/2658311.jpg" alt="notgettingenough " /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/103744127?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 06, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2658311-notgettingenough" class="user" title="notgettingenough ">notgettingenough </a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/119324.The_Subtle_Knife" title="Hardcover 9780679879251" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2658311-notgettingenough?shelf=childrens" class="actionLinkLite">childrens</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer103744127">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7398602063575061144">Later...<br /><br />It is so surprising to me that the thing I found vastly irritating right at page one of the first of this series - the daemon - so quickly captivated me. You have this daemon in you, all of us, just as the story goes. And as a child it is anything, it has the fantastical vision that children have, there is nothing to stop it. But then we mostly grow up and we mostly lose the idea that we can do anything, we lose imagination, we lose the unconscious bravery of our childhood, we lose the i</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7398602063575061144">Later...<br /><br />It is so surprising to me that the thing I found vastly irritating right at page one of the first of this series - the daemon - so quickly captivated me. You have this daemon in you, all of us, just as the story goes. And as a child it is anything, it has the fantastical vision that children have, there is nothing to stop it. But then we mostly grow up and we mostly lose the idea that we can do anything, we lose imagination, we lose the unconscious bravery of our childhood, we lose the intrepidness and curiosity with which we were born. And so our daemon can no longer be anything. It is a static reflection of the settled thing we become as we move into adulthood. Well, I cling to the idea that whether or not I've grown up, I nonetheless have a daemon which can be anything but I dare say that’s fanciful.<br /><br />Daemons die. They die because they were fighting for you, or because you couldn't fight hard enough for them, or because they are spurned - there are at least some things your daemon can be that thrive on the nourishment that is given them by others. You can't fight to save it because you can't force people see it the right way. They take away the thing that succoured your daemon and made it and you blossom, you see it lying on the ground, dying, and there is nothing you can do. You can't save it, only other people can.<br /><br />If you think about it, when you read these books, the reason you feel so utterly gutted whenever one of these creatures dies, is because you know what it feels like. You know that what is being described is exactly something dying in you, a process of loss that makes you a lesser person. Grey replaces lit-up, fear replaces joy, a sick pit in your stomach replaces a heart that beat too much from happiness. These things happen and in a heart-beat something infinitely precious is being severed from you. And I feel as helpless in their path as a small child watching something monstrously large taking their daemon away. And I guess like a small child I watch and hope something even bigger will come along and save us.<br /><br /><br />-----------------------<br /><br />A satire on the nature of academic research that one can only compare favourably with David Lodge’s work in this area. <br /><br />“‘Shadows are particles of consciousness. You ever heard anything so stupid? No wonder we can’t get our grant renewed.’….’It’s Dust,’ said Lyra authoritatively. ‘That’s what it is.’ ‘But you see, you can’t say this sort of thing in a funding application if you want to be taken seriously. It does not make sense. It cannot exist. It’s impossible, and if it isn’t impossible it’s irrelevant, amd if it isn’t either of those things it’s embarrassing.’….’Everything about this is <em>embarrassing,</em> she said. ‘D’you know how embarrassing it is to mention good and evil in a scientific laboratory? Have you any idea?’ One of the reasons I became a scientist was not to have to think about that kind of thing.’ ‘You’ve <em>got</em> to think about it,’ said Lyra severely.”<br /><br />Lyra, you see, is a child, so unlike research academics, she can have a plain interest in the truth.<br /><br />Later on, beginning p. 250 is a terribly amusing exchange between Dr Malone, trying to live up to the virtuous Lyra, her research associate who wants to take the money with the strings and Sir Charles, puller of the strings and more powerful than any piddly peer review. I love the part where he tries to seduce them with the lure of defence money if they tow the right line.<br /><br />But quite best of all, right near the end, the wonderful line of another child, Will, who, when a witch says ‘"No. No! That can’t be true. Impossible!"’ retorts so angrily with the simple clear mind of unaffected honesty: ‘"You think things have to be <em>possible</em>? Things have to be <em>true</em>"'. <br /><br />I would love to live by these words. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7398602063575061144">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_14076871">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/14076871" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/542037-annalisa" class="left imgcol" title="Annalisa"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1226336540p2/542037.jpg" alt="Annalisa" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/14076871?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 30, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/542037-annalisa" class="user" title="Annalisa">Annalisa</a>

        rated it

<span id="freeTextContainer12152003296328577134">What I did like about this book is that it starts with Lyra, a girl we have become acquainted with from another world, meeting Will, a boy from our world. Bringing the fantasy into our own reality was surreal and interesting. But only for a minute and then it became a bore. The story was slow and at some points stopped altogether to allow Pullman his theological preachings of anti-church and anti-god. If the story had been metaphorical I would have enjoyed it more, but it became less fiction and</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12152003296328577134">What I did like about this book is that it starts with Lyra, a girl we have become acquainted with from another world, meeting Will, a boy from our world. Bringing the fantasy into our own reality was surreal and interesting. But only for a minute and then it became a bore. The story was slow and at some points stopped altogether to allow Pullman his theological preachings of anti-church and anti-god. If the story had been metaphorical I would have enjoyed it more, but it became less fiction and more essay. <br /><br />In this book, Lyra and Will travel between worlds attempting to find his father and continue on with Lyra's mission. There are a lot of minor characters in too many places spreading the story out too thin to move quickly enough to keep one's attention. Eventually the action picks up as the characters merge, setting the scene for the major battle in book 3, but I was too annoyed with his writing by then to shrug it off. I almost didn't read the third book after this.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_18776388">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/18776388" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1022982-brad" class="left imgcol" title="Brad"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442484575p2/1022982.jpg" alt="Brad" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/18776388?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 25, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1022982-brad" class="user" title="Brad">Brad</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/119324.The_Subtle_Knife" title="Hardcover 9780679879251" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=young-adult" class="actionLinkLite">young-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer18776388">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1716019272105777177">The strangest thing about Phillip Pullman's <em>The Subtle Knife</em> is that it doesn't feel like the second book in a series, making me wonder whether Pullman first wrote this in conjunction with <em>The Amber Spyglass</em>, then wrote <em>The Golden Compass</em> as a prequel, which then became the first book in the series once they were published. <br /><br />Not that it matters. <br /><br />What matters is that <em>The Subtle Knife</em> is too fast, too plot driven, and too much "a set-up" book to be an effective second book in the trilogy. Second bo</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1716019272105777177">The strangest thing about Phillip Pullman's <em>The Subtle Knife</em> is that it doesn't feel like the second book in a series, making me wonder whether Pullman first wrote this in conjunction with <em>The Amber Spyglass</em>, then wrote <em>The Golden Compass</em> as a prequel, which then became the first book in the series once they were published. <br /><br />Not that it matters. <br /><br />What matters is that <em>The Subtle Knife</em> is too fast, too plot driven, and too much "a set-up" book to be an effective second book in the trilogy. Second books are generally strong because they give the reader a chance to breathe and get to know the characters. Not so in <em>The Subtle Knife</em>. Instead, Pullman introduces new characters, and not just peripheral characters, but Will, a character who seems to be the primary protagonist of <em>His Dark Materials</em>, and Mary, who will create the amber spyglass of the third book. <br /><br />Meanwhile, characters who seemed important in <em>The Golden Compass</em>, Azriel, Iorek and even Lyra are either supporting cast or merely spoken of. <br /><br />Perhaps this would work if the series was longer, if Pullman took more time with his stories, gave us greater detail, but he doesn't. Everything is fast, too fast, and the characters suffer, making it difficult to care about what is happening.<br /><br /><em>The Subtle Knife</em> is definitely a let down after Pullman's quite good <em>The Golden Compass</em>, and I have little hope for <em>The Amber Spyglass</em>, but I will know soon enough since I feel compelled to finish the series.<br /><br />One sidebar: for those who insist on calling this series "atheist," you should understand what atheism is. This series is anti-god, but that means the book MUST NOT be atheist. To be anti-god a story must posit a god, and by assuming the presence of a god (or god-like force) a story cannot be atheist. So there you go. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1716019272105777177">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer182914040">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15636407866718941721">As a second reading of this book, I was disappointed. I remember liking it much more when I first read it about 6 years ago. Pullman’s imagination is again shown off in the alternate worlds he creates, but the story did not grab me like The Golden Compass did. <br /><br />Lyra is so wonderfully written in The Golden Compass, but here she seems to be more ordinary and boring. I realize the author is trying to show her change and grow up, but all that happens is she gets dull. <br /><br />I miss also the detailed descrip</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15636407866718941721">As a second reading of this book, I was disappointed. I remember liking it much more when I first read it about 6 years ago. Pullman’s imagination is again shown off in the alternate worlds he creates, but the story did not grab me like The Golden Compass did. <br /><br />Lyra is so wonderfully written in The Golden Compass, but here she seems to be more ordinary and boring. I realize the author is trying to show her change and grow up, but all that happens is she gets dull. <br /><br />I miss also the detailed descriptions of the alethiometer. Pullman made the symbols seem so interesting in the first book, but here those details are missing. Instead Lyra uses it and results are given sans description of what symbols are involved. I know the book is supposed to focus on the knife, but still, why give up on the compass? <br /><br />I also found the shift in how powerful Lord Astriel &amp; Ms. Coulter are to be inconsistent with the first book. In The Golden Compass both are respected and powerful humans, but seem well within normal human limitations. In this book they seem to have become superhuman. Both suddenly knows how to command all kinds of beings and can do things other humans can’t. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15636407866718941721">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
